    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/541939-can-i-read-this-book-even-though-i-haven-t">Can I read this book even though I haven&#39;t read anything else in the Discworld series?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_541939'><a id="like_count_community_question_541939" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/541939?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F34517.Reaper_Man">like</a></span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/5903197-tim"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1311861358i/5903197._UX100_CR0,15,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/5903197-tim">Tim</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">No problem at all. I started my journey into Discworld with The Light Fantastic (book 2), even if it's a direct sequel to The Colour of Magic (book 1)<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">No problem at all. I started my journey into Discworld with The Light Fantastic (book 2), even if it's a direct sequel to The Colour of Magic (book 1) (you do have to read these books together). After that I read Hogfather, which came out much later. <br /><br />The other Discworld novels are written so that you can read them without worries and thus go back and forth, even if some characters come back here and there.<br /><br />So.. yes, you can read Reaper Man without prior knowledge. ;-)<br /><br />This overview, the last novel not added yet, can also help you find your way in the reading process: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://kokkieh.files.wordpress.com/2015/03/discworld-reading-order-guide.jpg">https://kokkieh.files.wordpress.com/2...</a><a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_2320523547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2320523547" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2320523547?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=pratchett-terry">pratchett-terry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2320523547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10943108575640823773">The second DEATH is as hilarious as the first one, this time positioning the friendly reaper in a perfect setting for having philosophical and deep thoughts about human nature and life in general.<br/><br/>The auditors are a mixture of bureaucracy, monotony, surveillance, and total order, each reader might find a subjective real life example for higher, creativity and life hating entities mostly interested in controlling everything, not even trusting each other, and hating generally anything different, ne</span>
  <span id="freeText10943108575640823773" style="display:none">The second DEATH is as hilarious as the first one, this time positioning the friendly reaper in a perfect setting for having philosophical and deep thoughts about human nature and life in general.<br /><br />The auditors are a mixture of bureaucracy, monotony, surveillance, and total order, each reader might find a subjective real life example for higher, creativity and life hating entities mostly interested in controlling everything, not even trusting each other, and hating generally anything different, new, or progressive. The way the are used in the DEATH novels is a good alternative theory for the real foundations of the physical laws, the universe, and anything, so string theory, behold and be aware! <br /><br />As I always say, I do know far too little about philosophy and mythology to get all the innuendos, inspirations, and connotations Pratchett is certainly using and many readers may find far more subtle, hidden, easter eggs than just the superficial humor I am able to identify.<br /><br />Shopping centers and consumerism, or in this case interdimensional city parasite malls, went a far way and evolution, similar to mallmargeddons in real life that became the last stage of an uncertain development since Pratchett wrote this one and it would be interesting to know how he would have satirized the big malls and megastores first eating away the rest of the food chain to finally be cannibalized by online trade.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I especially like the allegory of out of control superficial nihilistic money throwing temples detected by incompetent wizards of Unseen University. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I will highly subjectively and subliminally interpret is as out of control predator neoliberal capitalism randomly detected by ivory towers humanities who are unable to deal with a creature some of them helped constructing. And buy my stuff mostly online, because it´s so cheap, of course, hypocritical as I am, but organic at least. But it could also be seen as the warning of natural disaster or environmental destruction by scientists with not enough data, the impending doom of the manipulation of the laws of physics by science detected by science itself, etc. As always in Pratchett´s works, there are manifold ways to widen one´s horizon with the mind penetrating subtility he offers.<br /><br />Prepare for an example why the causality and paths of the universe shouldn´t be manipulated and what happens if DEATH doesn´t do his job anymore, such as zombie <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[rights groups suddenly finding more support and members. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I guess I should watch the movie Repo man, or read the script, to get more of the hidden humor related to the pun, although I don´t know if there are really elements of it in the novel, as I haven´t seen it. Tricky.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/ReaperMan">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a><br /><br />This one is added to all Pratchettian reviews:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibenwelt-Romane#Liste_der_Romane">https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibe...</a> <br />The idea of the dissected motifs rocks, highlighting the main real world inspirational elements of fiction and satire is something usually done with so called higher literature, but a much more interesting field in readable literature, as it offers the joy of reading, subtle criticism, and feeling smart all together</span>
  <a data-text-id="10943108575640823773" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5173053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2471394728146541415">Probably my favorite of all of the Discworld novels (and that's a hard choice, given my total love of the series). Of all of Pratchett's many quirky characters, I think I love Death the best, though I couldn't begin to tell you why. In this installment, Death gets fired and has to get a day job. In typical Discworld fashion, not everything goes according to plan.<br/><br/>Don't pay attention to the critics' quotes on the front of the book. Anyone who compares Pratchett to Tolkien or Douglas Adams hasn't a</span>
  <span id="freeText2471394728146541415" style="display:none">Probably my favorite of all of the Discworld novels (and that's a hard choice, given my total love of the series). Of all of Pratchett's many quirky characters, I think I love Death the best, though I couldn't begin to tell you why. In this installment, Death gets fired and has to get a day job. In typical Discworld fashion, not everything goes according to plan.<br /><br />Don't pay attention to the critics' quotes on the front of the book. Anyone who compares Pratchett to Tolkien or Douglas Adams hasn't actually read any of the three. It might be better to compare him to Jonathan Swift or Mark Twain; in spite of the dwarves, wizards, witches, werewolves, and other fantastic characters, Pratchett's novels are more a commentary on human nature &amp; society than high fantasy. Great fun!</span>

<div id="review_1261518814" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1261518814" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1261518814?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780753122129" href="/book/show/1011988.Reaper_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1261518814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10473191466539115294">Today would have been Sir Terry's 70th birthday.<br/>As some here know, I've put off reading the series because I still tear up when thinking of this great man and the terrible loss his death meant. Especially his books featuring Death as the main character are hard to read because their messages are hitting so close to home. Nevertheless, I agreed to read one book per month and this month, of all possible months, it was this novel's turn.<br/>Sometimes life just loves punching you in the gut.<br/>And yes, I h</span>
  <span id="freeText10473191466539115294" style="display:none">Today would have been Sir Terry's 70th birthday.<br>As some here know, I've put off reading the series because I still tear up when thinking of this great man and the terrible loss his death meant. Especially his books featuring Death as the main character are hard to read because their messages are hitting so close to home. Nevertheless, I agreed to read one book per month and this month, of all possible months, it was this novel's turn.<br>Sometimes life just loves punching you in the gut.<br>And yes, I half laughed hysterically, half cried pitifully while reading.<br><br>I've actually read <b>Reaper Man</b> before but it's been a long while and I must revise my original opinion: THIS is the best Death novel by Pratchett. Hands down.<br><br>The story is that some cosmic entities decide that the Discworld's Death has developed a personality and that sort of thing is simply not allowed. Therefore, he receives his very own hourglass and becomes mortal. Retired.<br>He decides to spend the time and see what life is all about. <br>In the meantime, the Discworld suffers some very weird incidents because once death isn't doing his job anymore, things start falling apart of course. Thus, people die but aren't dead, plants keep on growing and there are these little things that are plopping into existance everywhere (without people noticing).<br>In Ankh-Morpork, one of the settings, the wizards think it's the gods, the priests think it's magic and Lord Vetinari doesn't care who or what it is so long as order is restored again.<br>Windle Poons, a dying wizard, is trying to complete the process of dying, very hard even, but simply can't manage (not even when he gets buried by his colleagues). So he embarks on a journey, finding out what living really means and how much fun it can be - aided by a few other undead people.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524942019i/25449356.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Mrs Cake, a medium also living in the city, knows she has to tell someone what is going so horribly wrong, but learned never to trust any priests so who to turn to?!<br>Meanwhile, Death has become a farmhand and quite dedicated to his job.<br><br>We get some familiar characters such as the wizards and the Librarian, but many new ones are introduced as well (Lupin and Ludmila for example and wasn't that a sweet plot of Poons'?) and they immediately have found their way into my heart. SQUEAK! And we even get a pretty great romance in here! Seriously, if you want to know how to treat the woman you love, take notes from the Reaper himself.<br><br>This book is full of really profound messages about life and death and making your life count, if only by giving others some of your time (in any way this can be meant), religion, time in general, friendship, love ... Death is Pratchett's masterpiece although many of his other Discworld characters are fantastic as well. This novel, though extremely funny, might not be as hilarious as some of the others, but I have to say that due to the impeccable combination of profound messages, silly adventures and laugh-out-loud moments, it is simply perfect.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10473191466539115294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1936506725" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1936506725" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1936506725" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12845251550834003773">In 1976 Blue Oyster Cult sang <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=AUO_5EALZoM' rel='nofollow noopener'>Don’t Fear the Reaper</a>. In 1991, Sir Terry Pratchett first published his 11th Discworld novel Reaper Man. The comparison is mine, apparently Pratchett titled the book as a reference to Alex Cox’s 1984 fantasy film starring Emilio Estevez.<br/><br/>So, what happens when our Discworld friend Death is given a timer and … time to spend it? We get this fun book that helps us to revisit Discworld and some of our favorite Pratchett characters like Death, the Unseen Academy staff, Cut</span>
  <span id="freeText12845251550834003773" style="display:none">In 1976 Blue Oyster Cult sang <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=AUO_5EALZoM" rel="nofollow noopener">Don’t Fear the Reaper</a>. In 1991, Sir Terry Pratchett first published his 11th Discworld novel Reaper Man. The comparison is mine, apparently Pratchett titled the book as a reference to Alex Cox’s 1984 fantasy film starring Emilio Estevez.<br><br>So, what happens when our Discworld friend Death is given a timer and … time to spend it? We get this fun book that helps us to revisit Discworld and some of our favorite Pratchett characters like Death, the Unseen Academy staff, Cut Me Own Throat Dibbler, some of the City Watch and an Ankh-Morpork host of otherworldly Ghostbuster type shenanigans.<br><br>When Death takes a break out on the farm, all the spirits and ghosts and poltergeists and spooks back up with no where to go. Much of the book follows elderly wizard Windle Poons as he experiences more of life than he expected he had coming.<br><br>Funny as always, and insightful and clever and playfully satirical, in this we also see Pratchett’s unguarded and affectionate side. For Discworld fans.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1546865995i/26879169._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2468673132" />
    <a title="Adrian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22809267-adrian"><img alt="Adrian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484242635p2/22809267.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2468673132?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adrian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adrian" href="/user/show/22809267-adrian">Adrian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552134644" href="/book/show/833424.Reaper_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=read-again">read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=humorous">humorous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=fantasy-and-magic">fantasy-and-magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=zz-owned-books">zz-owned-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2468673132" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18355358268449673187">Wow, where’s the 6 star button <br/><br/>Ok , where should I start with this ? Some of TP's Discworld novels are great stories, full of humour with amazingly real and detailed characters that get into scrapes, have adventures, inherit kingdoms, fight wars in far off places (near the Rim ? ) And these books to me are 4 or 5 star reads depending on the humour content and the characters.<br/>And then, well yes, and then there are TP's books that are a parody of human life, allegorical masterpieces that mirror the</span>
  <span id="freeText18355358268449673187" style="display:none">Wow, where’s the 6 star button <br /><br />Ok , where should I start with this ? Some of TP's Discworld novels are great stories, full of humour with amazingly real and detailed characters that get into scrapes, have adventures, inherit kingdoms, fight wars in far off places (near the Rim ? ) And these books to me are 4 or 5 star reads depending on the humour content and the characters.<br />And then, well yes, and then there are TP's books that are a parody of human life, allegorical masterpieces that mirror the round-world we live upon, but these tales are told using some of the most amusing and amazing characters authors have ever created. <br />DEATH for instance, over the few books he has appeared in to date (I am reading the Discworld novels in order), DEATH has grown into a fully rounded (sic) character. Sir Terry has really got under his skin (oops sorry) and knows what makes him tick, how he reacts to certain situations, to such an extent that as you read he becomes real to the reader. You are there with him as he reaps the field, as he sharpens his scythe and as he rides away on Binky. <br />Ditto with the Unseen University Librarian, yes he only says Oook, but you begin to understand him. You want to share his world as he hunts for his next banana or as he delves into the magical depths of the library to find the one "tome" that could get the wizards out of the mess they're in.<br />I could go on about how his characters and stories mirror some of the pressing issues in OUR world, how through his tales he shows us OUR insensitivity, prejudice and bigotry, and that is all true, and also very important to realise, but primarily he is just an astonishing story teller.<br />This book does contain DEATH, the librarian, the Wizards and many more and is in my humble opinion (be it h'ever so 'umble) the best so far, a true masterpiece of literature, one that will remain with me along with probably another couple of dozen books that I have read over my 60 years and 78 days.<br /><br />As the French say "Chapeau" Sir Terry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18355358268449673187" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1940936145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1940936145" />
    <a title="Celise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4653644-celise"><img alt="Celise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546382482p2/4653644.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1940936145?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celise" href="/user/show/4653644-celise">Celise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552166683" href="/book/show/15796734-reaper-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4653644-celise?shelf=fantasy-adult">fantasy-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4653644-celise?shelf=owned-read">owned-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1940936145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1161416696044874799"><i>
  <b>“No one is actually dead until the ripples they cause in the world die away...”</b>
</i><br/><br/>"I want more Death" is what I spent most of this book thinking. 60% of this was about Windle Poons and other wizards and I just wasn't into the silly randomness of the problems they were dealing with. The "life force" issues felt too much like the alchemy issues in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10__Industrial_Revolution___1_' title='Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10; Industrial Revolution, #1) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Moving Pictures</a>, and I just couldn't get into that one.<br/><br/>I wanted to experience more of Death's life on the farm, and his whole side of things. I liked T</span>
  <span id="freeText1161416696044874799" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>“No one is actually dead until the ripples they cause in the world die away...”</b>
</i><br /><br />"I want more Death" is what I spent most of this book thinking. 60% of this was about Windle Poons and other wizards and I just wasn't into the silly randomness of the problems they were dealing with. The "life force" issues felt too much like the alchemy issues in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10__Industrial_Revolution___1_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10; Industrial Revolution, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a>, and I just couldn't get into that one.<br /><br />I wanted to experience more of Death's life on the farm, and his whole side of things. I liked The Death of Rats and The Death of Fleas as an added touch. A little nod to Mort or something may have been nice. <br /><br />This isn't bad, I just didn't get all that much out of it. It has my favourite cover (not the exact one on this edition, but close enough) so I was a little bit overexcited maybe? I don't know, I'm just a bit disappointed.</span>

    <a title="Aaron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82838-aaron"><img alt="Aaron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178904286p2/82838.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1163018?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron" href="/user/show/82838-aaron">Aaron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1163018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2259025535060072920">If I could steal credit for a book and claim it as my own, <i>Reaper Man</i> would most likely be it. This is the point, I think, where the Discworld novels stopped being entertaining light reading, and really became literature. It's a very subtle shift from the little parodies of science fiction and fantasy conventions of things like <i>The Light Fantastic</i> to the rich satire of a book like <i>Jingo</i>. Behind the story of Death being fired for having developed a personality, there's a clever little parable abo</span>
  <span id="freeText2259025535060072920" style="display:none">If I could steal credit for a book and claim it as my own, <i>Reaper Man</i> would most likely be it. This is the point, I think, where the Discworld novels stopped being entertaining light reading, and really became literature. It's a very subtle shift from the little parodies of science fiction and fantasy conventions of things like <i>The Light Fantastic</i> to the rich satire of a book like <i>Jingo</i>. Behind the story of Death being fired for having developed a personality, there's a clever little parable about what it means to be human, to be alive, and why the man locked in the tower watched the flight of birds.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2259025535060072920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_461009271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/461009271" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1170391-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590218363p2/1170391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/461009271?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/1170391-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781473200111" href="/book/show/19232680-reaper-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer461009271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16398087680145460532">Death has retired. Which might be a problem.<br/><br/>Death is by far my favourite character in the Discworld, and it feels especially poignant to read his stories now following the death of Terry Pratchett. There's just a small amount of melancholy that resonates from the pages. That said, I really enjoyed this and it made me laugh a fair few times. The wit and humour just can't be replicated by anyone else, and this just feels so British and wonderful.<br/><br/>The scenes with Bill Door are, obviously, my favouri</span>
  <span id="freeText16398087680145460532" style="display:none">Death has retired. Which might be a problem.<br /><br />Death is by far my favourite character in the Discworld, and it feels especially poignant to read his stories now following the death of Terry Pratchett. There's just a small amount of melancholy that resonates from the pages. That said, I really enjoyed this and it made me laugh a fair few times. The wit and humour just can't be replicated by anyone else, and this just feels so British and wonderful.<br /><br />The scenes with Bill Door are, obviously, my favourite - although I liked Ludmilla and Windle's stories too. The wizards I'm always inclined to like less, and their story does devolve into the more absurd. I like the more morally ambiguous dilemmas that Death faces. And he gets all the best lines and character interactions. The Death of Rats! Cyril the dyslexic chicken! If anything, I would have liked to have spent more time with Death and how he coped during his retirement.<br /><br />Possibly not quite as good as Mort, in my opinion, but I do love these novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16398087680145460532" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1920424027" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1920424027" />
    <a title="Emer (A Little Haze)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4629647-emer-a-little-haze"><img alt="Emer (A Little Haze)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589390599p2/4629647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1920424027?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emer (A Little Haze)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emer (A Little Haze)" href="/user/show/4629647-emer-a-little-haze">Emer (A Little Haze)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552134644" href="/book/show/833424.Reaper_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=read2017">read2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=2stars">2stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1920424027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2891382766502846126"><blockquote><i>Wizards don't believe in gods in the same way that most people don't find it necessary to believe in, say, tables. They know they're there, they know they're there for a purpose, they'd probably agree that they have a place in a well-organised universe, but they wouldn't see the point of believing, of going around saying "O great table, without whom we are as naught." Anyway, either the gods are there whether you believe in them or not, or exist only as a function of the belief, so either way yo</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2891382766502846126" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>Wizards don't believe in gods in the same way that most people don't find it necessary to believe in, say, tables. They know they're there, they know they're there for a purpose, they'd probably agree that they have a place in a well-organised universe, but they wouldn't see the point of believing, of going around saying "O great table, without whom we are as naught." Anyway, either the gods are there whether you believe in them or not, or exist only as a function of the belief, so either way you might as well ignore the whole business and, as it were, eat off your knees.<br /><br />Nevertheless, there is a small chapel off the University's Great Hall, because while the wizards stand right behind the philosophy as outlined above, you don't become a successful wizard by getting up gods' noses even if those noses only exist in an ethereal or metaphorical sense. Because while wizards don't believe in gods they know for a fact that </i>gods <i>believe in gods.</i></blockquote><br /><br />Reaper Man is the second book in the Discworld series that features Death as a main character. (Overall it is book 11) <br />I really wanted to love this. And at times I found it incredibly funny (case in point that first passage I quoted from). But this book dragged for me. I loved when Death was featured but I wasn't a fan of the storyline surrounding the secondary character, Wizard Windle Poons. <br /><br />Sadly I feel a bit underwhelmed by this but I will persist with reading these Discworld novels because I just know that one of them is going to become a favourite. The humour is too good to not want to read! I'm hoping that the next book in the Death series, Soul Music, will be more to my taste. <br /><br /><b>two and a half stars</b><br /><br />I did come across a quote perfectly suited to me and my penchant for abusing exclamation marks though! <br /><i>Five exclamation marks, the sure sign of an insane mind.</i><br />Yup! That's meeeee!!!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2891382766502846126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6918964257709874435"><b>...it's just that life is a habit that's hard to break...</b><br/><br/>With Death off gallivanting around, Discworld residents discover that eternal life ain't all it's cracked up to be. Others just want those dead people to <b>GO AWAY ALREADY!</b><br/><br/>Perhaps a support group is in order?<br/><br/>This entry in the series has EVERYTHING - action, adventure, thrills, chills, and romance, in addition to the usual amount of rampant silliness one has come to expect from Pratchett, who manages to prove once and for all that WORDS CAN H</span>
  <span id="freeText6918964257709874435" style="display:none"><b>...it's just that life is a habit that's hard to break...</b><br /><br />With Death off gallivanting around, Discworld residents discover that eternal life ain't all it's cracked up to be. Others just want those dead people to <b>GO AWAY ALREADY!</b><br /><br />Perhaps a support group is in order?<br /><br />This entry in the series has EVERYTHING - action, adventure, thrills, chills, and romance, in addition to the usual amount of rampant silliness one has come to expect from Pratchett, who manages to prove once and for all that WORDS CAN HURT YOU. Screw that old schoolyard saying! When all those "Buggers" you spout sprout wings and start stinging, you'll be sorry!<br /><br />The subject matter of this tale also lends itself to a bit more introspection and philosophical musings, and dare I say, a few nuggets of wisdom...<br /><br /><b>There was never anything to be gained from observing what humans said to one another - language was just there to hide their thoughts.</b><br /><br />Hmmm...</span>

  <span id="freeText10689423019741306611" style="display:none">1 Jan 2002 <br />6 Jul 2014<br />13 May 2016<br /><br />Is there anyone who doesn't love Death? <div id="review_611343389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/611343389" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/611343389?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552134644" href="/book/show/833424.Reaper_Man">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      Stewart
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer611343389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3562021336652084353"><b>Invasion of the Shopping Centres</b><br/>6 July 2013<br/><br/>	I guess the person that said that the problem with Terry Pratchett is that you have to read him more than once probably applied it to this book in the same way that it had been applied to the other books that I have read and discovered that the second time around I have enjoyed them much more than the first time. Okay, I am probably not going to pick up <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10_' title='Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Moving Pictures</a> again, though this one is definitely one that I should come back to again some time </span>
  <span id="freeText3562021336652084353" style="display:none"><b>Invasion of the Shopping Centres</b><br />6 July 2013<br /><br />	I guess the person that said that the problem with Terry Pratchett is that you have to read him more than once probably applied it to this book in the same way that it had been applied to the other books that I have read and discovered that the second time around I have enjoyed them much more than the first time. Okay, I am probably not going to pick up <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a> again, though this one is definitely one that I should come back to again some time down the track.<br /><br />	The problem that I found with this particular book is that Pratchett seems to try to squeeze two concurring plots into the single book, which is something that he has not really done previously. Most of his other books is generally focused on the main plot, where as this one deals with the retirement of Death and the resulting chaos that comes about, and concurrently deals with this strange phenomena that has appeared in Ankh-Morpork: Shopping Centres.<br /><br />	Somebody also suggested that Pratchett has moved from simply writing parodies of fantasy novels to becoming somewhat more satirical, though I would have to say that I think he has been like that for most of the Discworld series. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites__Discworld___3__Witches__1_" title="Equal Rites (Discworld, #3; Witches #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Equal Rites</a>, which deals with gender equality, was the third book in the series, and he returns to a similar issue with this book when dealing with discrimination against dead (or rather the undead) by the living.<br /><br />	Then there is this issue with shopping centres, which I sort of wonder what the point was, which is why I probably have to go back and read it again. It seems that these things first appear in the form of snow globes and then expand into miniature cities with hordes of vicious trolleys. It is also interesting how they have music which lures people into them in the same way that the Pied Piper lured rats, and children. Personally I believe that shopping centres are a lot more insidious, though you get the idea that from his books these aspects of modern culture are not all that pleasant. My gripe with shopping centres is the way they destroy public space and take people out of the public sphere and place them into the private sphere, which is in effect a controlled corporate space where the corporation not only sets the rules, but also controls what people see. In a way shopping centres destroy our culture and simply turn to what is in effect crass commercialism.<br /><br />	However, that is not the major plot of the book because the main plot deals with how Death is sacked from his job for becoming too human, and as such he retires to a small village where he takes up a job as a farm hand. This is where Pratchett's brilliance really comes out because of the utter banality of his existence, yet it is an existence that Death seems to enjoy. In a way, it is the banality, and being able to appreciate and love the banality of life, that makes us human.<br /><br />	Another thing is how Death is so obviously Death, but it is only the children that actually notice that he is a skeleton. It is obvious that he is a skeleton, and nothing actually changes when he goes into the ordinary world, yet nobody wants to accept it. In fact it seems that if we are confronted with something truly horrifying we end up putting it out of our mind and start pretending that it is actually something else. The reason that the children seem him for who he is: they have yet to learn that a creature such as death should be horrifying. In the end it all comes down to perception – a child's innocent prevents the child from experiencing fear, and it is only when the child learns to fear (or is told to fear something) that they actually begin to fear. In a way it is very much the same with us adults – if we are told to fear something, such as refugees, immigrants et al, then we begin to fear them, even if that fear is unfounded. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3562021336652084353" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer11655954781637025877">Just finished this whilst running and all the wonderful insightful thoughts I'd had about it over the past few weeks escape me as I sit exhausted yet happy. Reaper Man is the second novel that focuses on Death. This time he has been shorn of his anthropomorphic designation and sentenced to a short life on the Disc as punishment for becoming too individual. Those pesky Auditors make their Discworld debut (as do a whole raft of wonderfully fun creations including many new species in Reg Shoe's Fre</span>
  <span id="freeText11655954781637025877" style="display:none">Just finished this whilst running and all the wonderful insightful thoughts I'd had about it over the past few weeks escape me as I sit exhausted yet happy. Reaper Man is the second novel that focuses on Death. This time he has been shorn of his anthropomorphic designation and sentenced to a short life on the Disc as punishment for becoming too individual. Those pesky Auditors make their Discworld debut (as do a whole raft of wonderfully fun creations including many new species in Reg Shoe's Fresh Start Club for the not actually dead) and set about ridding the Disc of its meddling Death. It doesn't go as planned but it takes a certain talent to tell a story about death and dying and have the readers cheering for the figure with the scythe come the end and the journey with Bill Door is one of my favourites in the Discworld universe so far. Of course it wouldn't be an early Discworld novel without a simultaneous plot going on that ties in to the main story in some way, this time the recently invented wizard faculty get to raucously investigate what is happening to the world now that nothing is dying, including the suddenly full of beans Windle Poons, wizard - DECEASED.</span>

<div id="review_1259302081" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1259302081" />
    <a title="Lindsay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay"><img alt="Lindsay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588746288p2/10326608.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1259302081?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1259302081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15004263815218735935">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads' rel='nofollow noopener'>SpecFic Buddy Reads</a> group. I have no idea how many times I've read this book, as it's one of my favorites in the series and a comfort read for me. I think I last read this about five years ago, and my first was soon after it was first published.<br/><br/>The Auditors of reality have decided that Death should not have a personality and have decided to retire the incumbent. This results in an unemployed 7' tall skeleton with a magnificent white horse and a brand new lif</span>
  <span id="freeText15004263815218735935" style="display:none">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads" rel="nofollow noopener">SpecFic Buddy Reads</a> group. I have no idea how many times I've read this book, as it's one of my favorites in the series and a comfort read for me. I think I last read this about five years ago, and my first was soon after it was first published.<br /><br />The Auditors of reality have decided that Death should not have a personality and have decided to retire the incumbent. This results in an unemployed 7' tall skeleton with a magnificent white horse and a brand new life timer with very little time in it, and certain skills with a large farm implement. It also results in a period of chaos in Ankh-Morpork and elsewhere as the role of Death becomes vacant for a while. Which makes things inconvenient for the ancient wizard Windle Poons whose scheduled death doesn't quite happen as he expects, leaving him with the dubious help of the Unseen University faculty and the undead members of the Fresh Start Club.<br /><br />This is really two parallel stories that only meet tangentially. The story of 130-year old Windle Poons only beginning to live his life after his death is one. Death settling into his last days as the farmhand Bill Door on Miss Flitwick's farm is the other. It's the latter that makes this book a gem in the overall arc of the Discworld books, but it just wouldn't work nearly as well without the light note of the activity in Ankh-Morpork and the hilarious cast of characters that Windle has to deal with.<br /><br />Death gets to point out some things that are really profound throughout the series as a way of dispensing Terry's wisdom through an unassailable point of view, but he rarely gets to make profound statements about the role of Death himself. The statement he does make in this is one of the most memorable of the entire run.<br /><br />I've given Reaper Man five stars in the past, and while I've re-rated several books in this series during my reread, for this one those five stars get an underline and an exclamation mark. One of the greats of Pratchett's work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15004263815218735935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1054492803" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1054492803" />
    <a title="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5252054-irena"><img alt="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425497470p2/5252054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1054492803?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" href="/user/show/5252054-irena">ᴥ Irena ᴥ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6567829-reaper-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=comic-fantasy">comic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=zombies">zombies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=wizards-mages-or-sorcerers">wizards-mages-or-sorcerers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=vampires">vampires</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=werewolves">werewolves</a>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1054492803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9268099425603150876"><b>4.5</b><br/><blockquote>
  <b>'Inside Every Living Person is a Dead Person Waiting to Get Out...'</b>
</blockquote> Death is one of my favourite Discworld characters and <i>Reaper Man</i> is the second book in the <i>Death</i> novels. I prefer reading in general Discworld reading order though. Still, whether you read these books like I do, or you choose to read them as separate novels about Rincewind, or Death, or witches and so on, you will have lots of fun. <br/><br/>Death gets fired. Or something like that anyway. Instead of mopping around feeling sorry fo</span>
  <span id="freeText9268099425603150876" style="display:none"><b>4.5</b><br /><blockquote>
  <b>'Inside Every Living Person is a Dead Person Waiting to Get Out...'</b>
</blockquote> Death is one of my favourite Discworld characters and <i>Reaper Man</i> is the second book in the <i>Death</i> novels. I prefer reading in general Discworld reading order though. Still, whether you read these books like I do, or you choose to read them as separate novels about Rincewind, or Death, or witches and so on, you will have lots of fun. <br /><br />Death gets fired. Or something like that anyway. Instead of mopping around feeling sorry for himself (according to the ones who got him fired he shouldn't have taken the <i>he</i> part), he starts living, making friends, being a hero, learning things he only observed before and understanding them, and so on. <br /><br />Meanwhile in Ankh-Morpork, life force is growing, weird little globes are popping up all over the place and <i>things</i> act. Windle Poons, a hundred and thirty old wizard, dies but nobody comes to collect him. Death is not employed anymore. His colleagues try everything they can think of (including burying him in the crossroads), but nothing helps. Windle Poons is still there. He finds his way to a group of <i>life-challenged</i> individuals and there the adventure of his <i>unlife</i> starts.<br /><br />The troubles in Ankh-Morpork and Death's new experiences are the two threads we follow in <i>Reaper Man</i>, both funny and entertaining. Pratchett's greatest strength lies in those one liners he smartly sneaks in in every single story.<br />I would have liked more Death though.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9268099425603150876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18197582031197137096">Upon hearing the tragic news today, I have realized I have not added this book or written a review of it, and I feel truly bad about that.<br/><br/>If someone were to ask me which of Terry Pratchett's book is my favorite, I wouldn't hesitate. All of his books are works of art, but among them, this one stands out like a true masterpiece to me. I think this is the first book he wrote that has actually made me cry both in laughter and in sadness. It is the only one of his books that I have re-read so many ti</span>
  <span id="freeText18197582031197137096" style="display:none">Upon hearing the tragic news today, I have realized I have not added this book or written a review of it, and I feel truly bad about that.<br /><br />If someone were to ask me which of Terry Pratchett's book is my favorite, I wouldn't hesitate. All of his books are works of art, but among them, this one stands out like a true masterpiece to me. I think this is the first book he wrote that has actually made me cry both in laughter and in sadness. It is the only one of his books that I have re-read so many times and I know I will re-read it many more times in the future. <br /><br />Death has always been my favorite character, as he is, curiously, the most human and childlike, the most curious and without prejudice of all the others on Disc World. He is also the one I identified most as a child, a teenager and even now as a grown woman approaching her thirtieth birthday. I asked and still ask many of the same questions and find awe in many of the same simple things and the reason I do is, most of all, because of the genius of Terry Pratchett.<br /><br />I'm going to have to wrap this up here, because I've cried enough today and I'm starting to again.<br /><br />Thank you, sir Terry, for everything.</span>

<div id="review_1871415271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1871415271" />
    <a title="Esme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44110461-esme"><img alt="Esme" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871415271?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Esme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Esme" href="/user/show/44110461-esme">Esme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44110461-esme?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44110461-esme?shelf=light-fantasy">light-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871415271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11262798875167270626">EEEEEE!!!! This is one of my favorite Discworld books, it’s been in my top 5 Discworld books for a long time now, and I only seem to like it more every time I read it.<br/><br/>The Auditors of Reality are displeased that Death is starting to develop a “personality”. They are convinced that once one develops a personality, that personality must at some point come to an end – that’s the way individuals worked. In their opinion, only forces endure, it’s the way of things, Gravity having a sense of humor or c</span>
  <span id="freeText11262798875167270626" style="display:none">EEEEEE!!!! This is one of my favorite Discworld books, it’s been in my top 5 Discworld books for a long time now, and I only seem to like it more every time I read it.<br /><br />The Auditors of Reality are displeased that Death is starting to develop a “personality”. They are convinced that once one develops a personality, that personality must at some point come to an end – that’s the way individuals worked. In their opinion, only forces endure, it’s the way of things, Gravity having a sense of humor or caring about Humans would be unnatural. Now that Death is starting to be an individual has to become a mortal, whether he wants to or not.<br /><br />Death finds a new hourglass timer in his home which is set outside of reality and time. He had never seen it before, which is highly unusual since he’s the type of being to know exactly where everything is all the time. Misplacing things isn’t one of Death’s personality traits. Upon further inspection, he sees that his own name is on the timer, and realizes what happened however unlikely. The auditors come and explain that their predicament with his ‘personality’, and that Azreal has decided that he must die and be replaced by a new Death. So, Death loses his job, he didn’t even know that was in the realm of possibility. Not knowing what else to do, he heads back to the Discworld to live out his life as a mortal. He doesn’t know what to do with what Time he has left, Time, after all, has always been something that happened to other people, not Death. All things considered, he initially reacted well to being fired and was excited about “experiencing things” for the first time.<br /><br />Death eventually comes to work on a farm for Mrs. Flitworth, an older widow who needs help but can’t afford to pay him that much. He has to come up with a name for himself, so he chooses Bill Door, because of course he does. While living on the farm he experiences dreams for the first time, the sunlight and rain, new drinking companions. Over time he starts to dread his own certain death, like all mortals do. He gets more than he bargained for as far as life experiences, describing living as being pulled forward by darkness, and wedged between two cliffs. He doesn’t understand how mortals can stand being alive, knowing they’re going to die.<br /><br />Now that there’s no Death, Windle Poons can’t die, he’s 130 years old and ready for his next adventure in the afterlife and is very much hoping for a reincarnation. Most Wizards know exactly when they are going to die, they even make a celebration out of it similar to a birthday. All the Wizards gather round, have extremely awkward and forced conversation, avoiding all meaningful conversation as best they could, and try their best to make it through the evening. Wizards aren’t social creatures by nature. Windle gets rather disheartened that when it was his time to go, all that happened was his spirit left his body, and then re-entered his own body since there was no one to usher him to the other side.<br /><br />Most Wizards wait in nervous anticipation for their face to face meeting with the anthropomorphized personification of Death, but it’s even more unsettling when he doesn’t show up. No one knows what to make of his absence since it’s never happened before, and Wizards aren’t great at on-the-fly problem-solving. They’re a group that sits down and ponders for a bit, preferably with a Big Dinner. Since Death didn’t show up, it’s up to Windle to kill himself. He’s really giving it his best, among other things he tried burying himself and drowning himself. What’s bizarre is that he’s actually extremely healthy now, muscles that have ached or not worked for years are now working in overdrive. He doesn’t have any eyesight problems, his knees aren’t giving him shit – things are the best they’ve ever been, quite frankly. Windle eventually ends up joining an undead support group complete with an agoraphobic boogeyman.<br /><br />This is the first book that features the Death of Rats, since in Death’s absence each species on the Discworld has their own personified Death to take care of them, it’s the humans that are still waiting for their New Death, and while they wait, the Disc experiences an explosion of paranormal activity due to the excess life-force floating around.<br /><br />This is another book where I could just quote it all day long. I won’t though, for your sake. I identify with this book more and more as I get older, one of the main themes of the book is getting older, being old, or dying. Now that I work with the elderly/hospice this books calms me down rather well after a particularly shitty day.<br /><br />da4e49336353a27d00384d955572efa2.jpg<br /><br />Favorite Quotes<br /><br />“Death’s pale horse looked up from its oats and gave a little whinny of greeting. The horse’s name was Binky. He was a real horse. Death had tried fiery steeds and skeletal horses in the past, and found them impractical, especially the fiery ones, which tended to set light to their own bedding and stand in the middle of it looking embarrassed.”<br /><br />“Light thinks it travels faster than anything but it is wrong. No matter how fast light travels, it finds the darkness has always got there first, and is waiting for it.”<br /><br />“No one is actually dead until the ripples they cause in the world die away…”<br /><br />“I thought turning into a bat would be interesting, but the owls around here are murder”<br /><br />“Intellectually, Ridcully maintained his position for two reasons. One was that he never, ever, changed his mind about anything. The other was that it took him several minutes to understand any new idea put to him, and this is a very valuable trait in a leader, because anything anyone is still trying to explain to you after two minutes is probably important and anything they give up after a mere minute or so is almost certainly something they shouldn’t have been bothering you with in the first place.”<br /><br />“Was that what it was really like to be alive? The feeling of darkness dragging you forward? How could they live with it? And yet they did, and even seemed to find enjoyment in it, when surely the only sensible course would be to despair. Amazing. To feel you were a tiny living thing, sandwiched between two cliffs of darkness. How could they stand to be alive?”<br /><br />Audience:<br />comic fantasy<br />non human POV<br />stories about dying<br />high fantasy<br />shorter books<br />wizards/mages<br /><br /><br />Ratings:<br />Plot: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://13.25/15">13.25/15</a><br />Characters: 15/15<br />World Building: 15/15<br />Writing: 14/15<br />Pacing: 13/15<br />Originality: 13.5/15<br />Personal Enjoyment: 10/10<br /><br />Final Score: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://93.75/100">93.75/100</a> – 5/5 stars, highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11262798875167270626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_712882238" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/712882238" />
    <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers"><img alt="TheBookSmugglers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316717753p2/1223249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/712882238?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TheBookSmugglers" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers">TheBookSmugglers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1223249-thebooksmugglers?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1223249-thebooksmugglers?shelf=favorite-characters">favorite-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1223249-thebooksmugglers?shelf=fantasy-awesomeness">fantasy-awesomeness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1223249-thebooksmugglers?shelf=read-all-the-terry-pratchetts">read-all-the-terry-pratchetts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer712882238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2269388076307189408">Original review posted on <a href='http://thebooksmugglers.com/2013/09/old-school-wednesdays-reaper-man-by-terry-pratchett.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book Smugglers</a><br/><br/>Reaper Man is a book that I find really hard to define. It would be so easy to simply describe it as a hilarious – or even absurdist – romp about the chaos that ensues after Death is (forcibly) retired without an immediate replacement to take over his duties. There are wizards running around, there are snow globes that pop out of nowhere then hatch into EVIL shopping trolleys (!!), a bunch of previously-undead people (including a vampire and a boogeyma</span>
  <span id="freeText2269388076307189408" style="display:none">Original review posted on <a href="http://thebooksmugglers.com/2013/09/old-school-wednesdays-reaper-man-by-terry-pratchett.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book Smugglers</a><br /><br />Reaper Man is a book that I find really hard to define. It would be so easy to simply describe it as a hilarious – or even absurdist – romp about the chaos that ensues after Death is (forcibly) retired without an immediate replacement to take over his duties. There are wizards running around, there are snow globes that pop out of nowhere then hatch into EVIL shopping trolleys (!!), a bunch of previously-undead people (including a vampire and a boogeyman) fighting for Undead Rights alongside the newly-undead Windle Poons. Whilst, in the meantime, basically oblivious to all the chaos, Death assumes the mantle of Bill Door and finds work at Miss Flitworth’s farm as a literal reaper man, helping with the harvest.<br /><br />And this description above would not be inexact.<br /><br />But it would be an incomplete one. This is a funny, absurd book and it does feature all of the above and it is perfectly fun and fine at face value. But it is finer when it comes to its metaphors and its thought-provoking themes. There is actually one fun moment of meta-awareness within the story when Windle Poons realises that the whole chaos surrounding him, the things happening to him are so full of metaphorical interpretations it is almost too much.<br /><br />Anyways, one of the main ideas here is that when Death retires, the living cease to die – at least for a time. In a more immediate storyline, the building of this untapped life force causes many problems for the inhabitants of Discworld and allows for darker forces to try to exploit this gap.<br /><br />The point is, no Death = no moving on, especially to those who are waiting for this idea of personified Death to come and collect them. Actually one of the recurrent ideas of the Death books (at least these two I have read) is that beliefis a force to be reckoned with and it infuses the entire concept of this worldbuilding.<br /><br />One of the main characters here alongside Death is the elderly magician Widdle Poons who have lived an unremarkable life until he became undead. He died but there was no one to collect him so he came back to life only to find that he now has a strong body and a clear mind for the first time in decades. This extra time proves to be everything to him – at first, he is perfectly happy to go along with his friends’ plans to rebury him (alive) because he understands his being HERE is completely abnormal. But then he finds himself being needed…and that is a powerful motivator.<br /><br />Meanwhile, Death finds himself with counted time for the first time. For someone who never had to count time before, this comes as a shock. It is the first time since the beginning of life that Death truly understands his harvest.<br /><br />This juxtaposition of both storylines is fascinating because what happened to both these characters is a boon but in different ways. Widdle has more time, Death has less time but both find meaning. And it’s remarkable because it is still all so relative because minutes and hours and lives are still ephemeral in the great scheme of things but all so important to everyone.<br /><br />So this is about Time and about life and about being needed, being helpful. It’s also about empathy: the ending of this book is one of the most beautiful things I have ever read. It’s Death coming to a better understanding of his role, of his standing, of the humans he is supposed to harvest. His relationship with Miss Flitworth is a thing of heart-warming beauty and their final moments and their literal danse macabre are so full of compassion it almost made my heart burst.<br /><br />And it is funny because Death becomes even more humanised – which is exactly what caused the problem in the first place – but he is now probably a better Death than ever before. You just ask the Death of Rats (and while at it, the Death of Fleas) what they think of it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2269388076307189408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
